/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>; 7 February 1812&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 9 June 1870) was an English writer and social critic. He created some of the world\'s best-known fictional characters and is regarded by many as the greatest novelist of the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the 20th century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories are still widely read today.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>, Dickens left school to work in a factory when his father was incarcerated in a <a href="/wiki/Debtors%27_prison" title="Debtors&#39; prison">debtors\' prison</a>. Despite his lack of formal education, he edited a weekly journal for 20 years, wrote 15 novels, five <a href="/wiki/Novellas" class="mw-redirect" title="Novellas">novellas</a>, hundreds of short stories and non-fiction articles, lectured and performed <a href="/wiki/Oral_interpretation" title="Oral interpretation">readings</a> extensively, was an indefatigable letter writer, and campaigned vigorously for children\'s rights, education, and other social reforms.\n</p><p>Dickens\'s literary success began with the 1836 serial publication of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pickwick_Papers" title="The Pickwick Papers">The Pickwick Papers</a></i>. Within a few years he had become an international literary celebrity, famous for his humour, satire, and keen observation of character and society. His novels, most published in monthly or weekly instalments, pioneered the <a href="/wiki/Serial_(literature)" title="Serial (literature)">serial</a> publication of narrative fiction, which became the dominant Victorian mode for novel publication.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lodge_2002_118_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lodge_2002_118-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cliffhanger" title="Cliffhanger">Cliffhanger</a> endings in his serial publications kept readers in suspense.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The instalment format allowed Dickens to evaluate his audience\'s reaction, and he often modified his plot and character development based on such feedback.<sup id="cite_ref-Lodge_2002_118_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lodge_2002_118-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> For example, when his wife\'s <a href="/wiki/Chiropodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiropodist">chiropodist</a> expressed distress at the way Miss Mowcher in <i><a href="/wiki/David_Copperfield" title="David Copperfield">David Copperfield</a></i> seemed to reflect her disabilities, Dickens improved the character with positive features.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His plots were carefully constructed, and he often wove elements from topical events into his narratives.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Masses of the illiterate poor chipped in <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British pre-decimal coin)">ha\'pennies</a> to have each new monthly episode read to them, opening up and inspiring a new class of readers.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens was regarded as the literary colossus of his age.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> His 1843 novella, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Christmas_Carol" title="A Christmas Carol">A Christmas Carol</a></i>, remains popular and continues to inspire adaptations in every artistic genre. <i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist" title="Oliver Twist">Oliver Twist</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations">Great Expectations</a></i> are also frequently adapted, and, like many of his novels, evoke images of early Victorian London. His 1859 novel, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="A Tale of Two Cities">A Tale of Two Cities</a></i>, set in London and Paris, is his best-known work of historical fiction. Dickens has been praised by fellow writers\xe2\x80\x94from <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> to <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Wolfe" title="Tom Wolfe">Tom Wolfe</a> \xe2\x80\x94for his <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realism</a>, comedy, prose style, unique characterisations, and <a href="/wiki/Child_labour#Historical" title="Child labour">social criticism</a>. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> complained of a lack of psychological depth, loose writing, and a vein of saccharine sentimentalism. The term <i>Dickensian</i> is used to describe something that is reminiscent of Dickens and his writings, such as poor social conditions or comically repulsive characters.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Farewell_readings"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Farewell readings</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Literary_style"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Literary style</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Characters"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Characters</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Autobiographical_elements"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Autobiographical elements</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Episodic_writing"><span class="tocnumber">11.3</span> <span class="toctext">Episodic writing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Social_commentary"><span class="tocnumber">11.4</span> <span class="toctext">Social commentary</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Literary_techniques"><span class="tocnumber">11.5</span> <span class="toctext">Literary techniques</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Reception"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Reception</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Influence_and_legacy"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Influence and legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Notable_works"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Notable works</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Works"><span class="tocnumber">17.1</span> <span class="toctext">Works</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Organisations_and_portals"><span class="tocnumber">17.2</span> <span class="toctext">Organisations and portals</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Museums"><span class="tocnumber">17.3</span> <span class="toctext">Museums</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Other"><span class="tocnumber">17.4</span> <span class="toctext">Other</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG/220px-CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG/330px-CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG/440px-CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesDickens_house_Portsmouth.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles Dickens\'s birthplace, 393 Commercial Road, Portsmouth</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg/220px-ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg/330px-ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg/440px-ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="489" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChathamOrdnanceTerrCrop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2 Ordnance Terrace, <a href="/wiki/Chatham,_Medway" class="mw-redirect" title="Chatham, Medway">Chatham</a>, Dickens\'s home 1817&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 May 1821<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Charles John Huffam Dickens was born on 7 February 1812, at 1 Mile End Terrace (now 393 Commercial Road), <a href="/wiki/Landport" title="Landport">Landport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Portsea_Island" title="Portsea Island">Portsea Island</a> (<a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>), the second of eight children of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Dickens" title="Elizabeth Dickens">Elizabeth Dickens</a> (n\xc3\xa9e Barrow; 1789\xe2\x80\x931863) and <a href="/wiki/John_Dickens" title="John Dickens">John Dickens</a> (1785\xe2\x80\x931851). His father was a clerk in the Navy Pay Office and was temporarily stationed in the district. He asked Christopher Huffam,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1999_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1999-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> rigger to His Majesty\'s Navy, gentleman, and head of an established firm, to act as godfather to Charles. Huffam is thought to be the inspiration for Paul Dombey, the owner of a shipping company in Dickens\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Dombey_and_Son" title="Dombey and Son">Dombey and Son</a></i> (1848).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1999_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1999-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 1815, John Dickens was called back to London, and the family moved to Norfolk Street, <a href="/wiki/Fitzrovia" title="Fitzrovia">Fitzrovia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> When Charles was four, they relocated to <a href="/wiki/Sheerness" title="Sheerness">Sheerness</a>, and thence to <a href="/wiki/Chatham,_Medway" class="mw-redirect" title="Chatham, Medway">Chatham</a>, Kent, where he spent his formative years until the age of 11. His early life seems to have been idyllic, though he thought himself a "very small and not-over-particularly-taken-care-of boy".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Charles spent time outdoors, but also read voraciously, including the <a href="/wiki/Picaresque_novel" title="Picaresque novel">picaresque novels</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Smollett" title="Tobias Smollett">Tobias Smollett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding" title="Henry Fielding">Henry Fielding</a>, as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Robinson_Crusoe" title="Robinson Crusoe">Robinson Crusoe</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gil_Blas" title="Gil Blas">Gil Blas</a></i>. He read and reread <i><a href="/wiki/The_Arabian_Nights" class="mw-redirect" title="The Arabian Nights">The Arabian Nights</a></i> and the Collected Farces of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Inchbald" title="Elizabeth Inchbald">Elizabeth Inchbald</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He retained poignant memories of childhood, helped by an excellent memory of people and events, which he used in his writing.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> His father\'s brief work as a clerk in the Navy Pay Office afforded him a few years of private education, first at a <a href="/wiki/Dame_school" title="Dame school">dame school</a>, and then at a school run by William Giles, a <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">dissenter</a>, in Chatham.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="drawing" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg/170px-Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg/255px-Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg/340px-Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1057" data-file-height="1479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens-at-the-Blacking-Warehouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration by Fred Bernard of Dickens at work in a shoe-blacking factory after his father had been sent to the <a href="/wiki/Marshalsea" title="Marshalsea">Marshalsea</a>, published in the 1892 edition of Forster\'s <i>Life of Dickens</i><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>This period came to an end in June 1822, when John Dickens was recalled to Navy Pay Office headquarters at <a href="/wiki/Somerset_House" title="Somerset House">Somerset House</a>, and the family (except for Charles, who stayed behind to finish his final term of work) moved to <a href="/wiki/Camden_Town" title="Camden Town">Camden Town</a> in London.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The family had left Kent amidst rapidly mounting debts, and, living beyond his means,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> John Dickens was forced by his creditors into the <a href="/wiki/Marshalsea" title="Marshalsea">Marshalsea</a> <a href="/wiki/Debtors%27_prison" title="Debtors&#39; prison">debtors\' prison</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a>, London in 1824. His wife and youngest children joined him there, as was the practice at the time. Charles, then 12 years old, boarded with Elizabeth Roylance, a family friend, at 112 College Place, Camden Town.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Roylance was "a reduced [impoverished] old lady, long known to our family", whom Dickens later immortalised, "with a few alterations and embellishments", as "Mrs. Pipchin" in <i>Dombey and Son</i>. Later, he lived in a back-attic in the house of an agent for the <a href="/wiki/Insolvency" title="Insolvency">Insolvent Court</a>, Archibald Russell, "a fat, good-natured, kind old gentleman... with a quiet old wife" and lame son, in <a href="/wiki/Lant_Street" title="Lant Street">Lant Street</a> in Southwark.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> They provided the inspiration for the Garlands in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Curiosity_Shop" title="The Old Curiosity Shop">The Old Curiosity Shop</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On Sundays\xe2\x80\x94with his sister Frances, free from her studies at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Music" title="Royal Academy of Music">Royal Academy of Music</a>\xe2\x80\x94he spent the day at the Marshalsea.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Dickens later used the prison as a setting in <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Dorrit" title="Little Dorrit">Little Dorrit</a></i>. To pay for his board and to help his family, Dickens was forced to leave school and work ten-hour days at Warren\'s <a href="/wiki/Shoe_polish" title="Shoe polish">Blacking</a> Warehouse, on Hungerford Stairs, near the present <a href="/wiki/Charing_Cross_railway_station" title="Charing Cross railway station">Charing Cross railway station</a>, where he earned six <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> a week pasting labels on pots of boot blacking. The strenuous and often harsh working conditions made a lasting impression on Dickens and later influenced his fiction and essays, becoming the foundation of his interest in the reform of socio-economic and labour conditions, the rigours of which he believed were unfairly borne by the poor. He later wrote that he wondered "how I could have been so easily cast away at such an age".<sup id="cite_ref-Foster23_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foster23-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> As he recalled to <a href="/wiki/John_Forster_(biographer)" title="John Forster (biographer)">John Forster</a> (from <i>The Life of Charles Dickens</i>):\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The blacking-warehouse was the last house on the left-hand side of the way, at old Hungerford Stairs. It was a crazy, tumble-down old house, abutting of course on the river, and literally overrun with rats. Its wainscoted rooms, and its rotten floors and staircase, and the old grey rats swarming down in the cellars, and the sound of their squeaking and scuffling coming up the stairs at all times, and the dirt and decay of the place, rise up visibly before me, as if I were there again. The counting-house was on the first floor, looking over the coal-barges and the river. There was a recess in it, in which I was to sit and work. My work was to cover the pots of paste-blacking; first with a piece of oil-paper, and then with a piece of blue paper; to tie them round with a string; and then to clip the paper close and neat, all round, until it looked as smart as a pot of ointment from an apothecary\'s shop. When a certain number of grosses of pots had attained this pitch of perfection, I was to paste on each a printed label, and then go on again with more pots. Two or three other boys were kept at similar duty down-stairs on similar wages. One of them came up, in a ragged apron and a paper cap, on the first Monday morning, to show me the trick of using the string and tying the knot. His name was Bob Fagin; and I took the liberty of using his name, long afterwards, in Oliver Twist.<sup id="cite_ref-Foster23_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foster23-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>When the warehouse was moved to Chandos Street in the smart, busy district of <a href="/wiki/Covent_Garden" title="Covent Garden">Covent Garden</a> the boys worked in a room in which the window gave onto the street and little audiences gathered and watched them at work\xe2\x80\x94in Dickens biographer <a href="/wiki/Simon_Callow" title="Simon Callow">Simon Callow</a>\'s estimation, the public display was "a new refinement added to his misery".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison,_1897_(2).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison%2C_1897_%282%29.png/170px-Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison%2C_1897_%282%29.png" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison%2C_1897_%282%29.png/255px-Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison%2C_1897_%282%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison%2C_1897_%282%29.png/340px-Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison%2C_1897_%282%29.png 2x" data-file-width="788" data-file-height="1198" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Courtyard_of_the_former_Marshalsea_prison,_1897_(2).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Marshalsea" title="Marshalsea">Marshalsea</a> around 1897, after it had closed</div></div></div>\n<p>A few months after his imprisonment, John Dickens\'s mother, Elizabeth Dickens, died and bequeathed him \xc2\xa3450. On the expectation of this legacy, Dickens was released from prison. Under the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Insolvent Debtors Act</a>, Dickens arranged for payment of his creditors, and he and his family left Marshalsea,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> for the home of Mrs Roylance.\n</p><p>Charles\'s mother, Elizabeth Dickens, did not immediately support his removal from the boot-blacking warehouse. This influenced Dickens\'s view that a father should rule the family, and a mother find her proper sphere inside the home: "I never afterwards forgot, I never shall forget, I never can forget, that my mother was warm for my being sent back". His mother\'s failure to request his return was a factor in his dissatisfied attitude towards women.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Righteous indignation stemming from his own situation and the conditions under which <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working-class</a> people lived became major themes of his works, and it was this unhappy period in his youth to which he alluded in his favourite, and most <a href="/wiki/Autobiographical_novel" title="Autobiographical novel">autobiographical, novel</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/David_Copperfield" title="David Copperfield">David Copperfield</a></i>:<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> "I had no advice, no counsel, no encouragement, no consolation, no assistance, no support, of any kind, from anyone, that I can call to mind, as I hope to go to heaven!"<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson61_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson61-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens was eventually sent to the Wellington House Academy in <a href="/wiki/Camden_Town" title="Camden Town">Camden Town</a>, where he remained until March 1827, having spent about two years there. He did not consider it to be a good school: "Much of the haphazard, desultory teaching, poor discipline punctuated by the headmaster\'s sadistic brutality, the seedy ushers and general run-down atmosphere, are embodied in Mr. Creakle\'s Establishment in <i>David Copperfield</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson61_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson61-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens worked at the law office of Ellis and Blackmore, attorneys, of Holborn Court, <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Inn" title="Gray&#39;s Inn">Gray\'s Inn</a>, as a junior <a href="/wiki/Law_clerk" title="Law clerk">clerk</a> from May 1827 to November 1828. He was a gifted mimic and impersonated those around him: clients, lawyers, and clerks. He went to theatres obsessively\xe2\x80\x94he claimed that for at least three years he went to the theatre every single day. His favourite actor was <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mathews" title="Charles Mathews">Charles Mathews</a>, and Dickens learnt his <i>monopolylogues</i>, (farces in which Mathews played every character), by heart.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Then, having learned <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gurney_(shorthand_writer)" title="Thomas Gurney (shorthand writer)">Gurney\'s</a> system of shorthand in his spare time, he left to become a freelance reporter. A distant relative, Thomas Charlton, was a freelance reporter at <a href="/wiki/Doctors%27_Commons" title="Doctors&#39; Commons">Doctors\' Commons</a>, and Dickens was able to share his box there to report the legal proceedings for nearly four years.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This education was to inform works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Nickleby" title="Nicholas Nickleby">Nicholas Nickleby</a></i>, <i>Dombey and Son</i>, and especially <i>Bleak House</i>\xe2\x80\x94whose vivid portrayal of the machinations and bureaucracy of the legal system did much to enlighten the general public and served as a vehicle for dissemination of Dickens\'s own views regarding, particularly, the heavy burden on the poor who were forced by circumstances to "go to law".\n</p><p>In 1830, Dickens met his first love, Maria Beadnell, thought to have been the model for the character Dora in <i>David Copperfield</i>. Maria\'s parents disapproved of the courtship and ended the relationship by sending her to school in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Journalism_and_early_novels">Journalism and early novels</span></h2>\n<p>In 1832, at age 20, Dickens was energetic and increasingly self-confident.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> He enjoyed mimicry and popular entertainment, lacked a clear, specific sense of what he wanted to become, and yet knew he wanted fame. Drawn to the theatre\xe2\x80\x94he became an early member of the <a href="/wiki/Garrick_Club" title="Garrick Club">Garrick</a><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94he landed an acting audition at Covent Garden, where the manager <a href="/wiki/George_Bartley_(comedian)" title="George Bartley (comedian)">George Bartley</a> and the actor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kemble" title="Charles Kemble">Charles Kemble</a> were to see him. Dickens prepared meticulously and decided to imitate the comedian Charles Mathews, but ultimately he missed the audition because of a cold. Before another opportunity arose, he had set out on his career as a writer.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 1833 he submitted his first story, "A Dinner at Poplar Walk", to the London periodical <i><a href="/wiki/Monthly_Magazine" title="Monthly Magazine">Monthly Magazine</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Patten(2001)16ff_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patten(2001)16ff-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> William Barrow, a brother of his mother, offered him a job on <i>The Mirror of Parliament</i> and he worked in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> for the first time early in 1832. He rented rooms at <a href="/wiki/Furnival%27s_Inn" title="Furnival&#39;s Inn">Furnival\'s Inn</a> and worked as a political journalist, reporting on <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliamentary</a> debates, and he travelled across Britain to cover election campaigns for the <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Chronicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Morning Chronicle">Morning Chronicle</a></i>. His journalism, in the form of sketches in periodicals, formed his first collection of pieces, published in 1836: <i><a href="/wiki/Sketches_by_Boz" title="Sketches by Boz">Sketches by Boz</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94Boz being a family nickname he employed as a pseudonym for some years.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glancy_1999_6_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glancy_1999_6-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Dickens apparently adopted it from the nickname "Moses", which he had given to his youngest brother <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Dickens" title="Augustus Dickens">Augustus Dickens</a>, after a character in Oliver Goldsmith\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Vicar_of_Wakefield" title="The Vicar of Wakefield">The Vicar of Wakefield</a></i>. When pronounced by anyone with a head cold, "Moses" became "Boses"\xe2\x80\x94later shortened to <i>Boz</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Glancy_1999_6_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glancy_1999_6-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s own name was considered "queer" by a contemporary critic, who wrote in 1849: "Mr Dickens, as if in revenge for his own queer name, does bestow still queerer ones upon his fictitious creations." He contributed to and edited journals throughout his literary career.<sup id="cite_ref-Patten(2001)16ff_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patten(2001)16ff-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In January 1835 the <i>Morning Chronicle</i> launched an evening edition, under the editorship of the <i>Chronicle\'</i>s music critic, <a href="/wiki/George_Hogarth" title="George Hogarth">George Hogarth</a>. Hogarth invited Dickens to contribute <i>Street Sketches</i> and Dickens became a regular visitor to his Fulham house, excited by Hogarth\'s friendship with a hero of his, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>, and enjoying the company of Hogarth\'s three daughters\xe2\x80\x94Georgina, Mary, and nineteen-year-old Catherine.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Catherine_Dickens.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Catherine_Dickens.jpg/170px-Catherine_Dickens.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Catherine_Dickens.jpg/255px-Catherine_Dickens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Catherine_Dickens.jpg/340px-Catherine_Dickens.jpg 2x" data-file-width="346" data-file-height="432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Catherine_Dickens.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Catherine Hogarth Dickens by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Lawrence" title="Samuel Lawrence">Samuel Lawrence</a> (1838)</div></div></div>\n<p>Dickens made rapid progress both professionally and socially. He began a friendship with <a href="/wiki/William_Harrison_Ainsworth" title="William Harrison Ainsworth">William Harrison Ainsworth</a>, the author of the highwayman novel <i><a href="/wiki/Rookwood_(novel)" title="Rookwood (novel)">Rookwood</a></i> (1834), whose bachelor salon in <a href="/wiki/Harrow_Road" title="Harrow Road">Harrow Road</a> had become the meeting place for a set that included <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Maclise" title="Daniel Maclise">Daniel Maclise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bulwer-Lytton" title="Edward Bulwer-Lytton">Edward Bulwer-Lytton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Cruikshank" title="George Cruikshank">George Cruikshank</a>. All these became his friends and collaborators, with the exception of Disraeli, and he met his first publisher, John Macrone, at the house.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The success of <i>Sketches by Boz</i> led to a proposal from publishers <a href="/wiki/Chapman_and_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Chapman and Hall">Chapman and Hall</a> for Dickens to supply text to match <a href="/wiki/Robert_Seymour_(illustrator)" title="Robert Seymour (illustrator)">Robert Seymour</a>\'s engraved illustrations in a monthly <a href="/wiki/Letterpress_printing" title="Letterpress printing">letterpress</a>. Seymour committed suicide after the second instalment, and Dickens, who wanted to write a connected series of sketches, hired "<a href="/wiki/Hablot_Knight_Browne" title="Hablot Knight Browne">Phiz</a>" to provide the engravings (which were reduced from four to two per instalment) for the story. The resulting story became <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pickwick_Papers" title="The Pickwick Papers">The Pickwick Papers</a></i>, and though the first few episodes were not successful, the introduction of the Cockney character <a href="/wiki/Sam_Weller_(character)" title="Sam Weller (character)">Sam Weller</a> in the fourth episode (the first to be illustrated by Phiz) marked a sharp climb in its popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The final instalment sold 40,000 copies.<sup id="cite_ref-Patten(2001)16ff_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patten(2001)16ff-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1836 Dickens accepted the position of editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Bentley%27s_Miscellany" title="Bentley&#39;s Miscellany">Bentley\'s Miscellany</a></i>, a position he held for three years, until he fell out with the owner.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In 1836 as he finished the last instalments of <i>The Pickwick Papers</i>, he began writing the beginning instalments of <i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist" title="Oliver Twist">Oliver Twist</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94writing as many as 90 pages a month\xe2\x80\x94while continuing work on <i>Bentley\'s</i> and also writing four plays, the production of which he oversaw. <i>Oliver Twist,</i> published in 1838, became one of Dickens\'s better known stories, and was the first Victorian novel with a child <a href="/wiki/Protagonist" title="Protagonist">protagonist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smiley12ff_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smiley12ff-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg/170px-Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg/255px-Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg/340px-Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3071" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_by_Daniel_Maclise.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young Charles Dickens by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Maclise" title="Daniel Maclise">Daniel Maclise</a>, 1839</div></div></div>\n<p>On 2 April 1836, after a one-year engagement, and between episodes two and three of <i>The Pickwick Papers</i>, Dickens married <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Dickens" title="Catherine Dickens">Catherine Thomson Hogarth</a> (1816\xe2\x80\x931879), the daughter of George Hogarth, editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Evening_Chronicle" title="Evening Chronicle">Evening Chronicle</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlicke1999_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlicke1999-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> They were married in St. Luke\'s Church,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a>, London. After a brief honeymoon in <a href="/wiki/Chalk,_Kent" title="Chalk, Kent">Chalk</a> in Kent the couple returned to lodgings at <a href="/wiki/Furnival%27s_Inn" title="Furnival&#39;s Inn">Furnival\'s Inn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The first of their <a href="/wiki/Dickens_family" title="Dickens family">ten children</a>, Charley, was born in January 1837, and a few months later the family set up <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens_Museum,_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Dickens Museum, London">home in Bloomsbury</a> at 48 Doughty Street, London, (on which Charles had a three-year lease at \xc2\xa380 a year) from 25 March 1837 until December 1839.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlicke1999_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlicke1999-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s younger brother <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Dickens" title="Frederick Dickens">Frederick</a> and Catherine\'s 17-year-old sister Mary, moved in with them. Dickens became very attached to Mary, and she died in his arms after a brief illness in 1837. Unusually for Dickens, as a consequence of his shock, he stopped working, and he and Kate stayed at a little farm on <a href="/wiki/Hampstead_Heath" title="Hampstead Heath">Hampstead Heath</a> for a fortnight. Dickens idealised Mary- the character he fashioned after her, <a href="/wiki/Rose_Maylie" title="Rose Maylie">Rose Maylie</a>, he found he could not now kill, as he had planned, in his fiction<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and according to Ackroyd he drew on memories of her for his later descriptions of <a href="/wiki/Little_Nell_(Dickens)" class="mw-redirect" title="Little Nell (Dickens)">Little Nell</a> and Florence Dombey.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> His grief was so great that he was unable to meet the deadline for the June instalment of <i>Pickwick Papers</i> and had to cancel the <i>Oliver Twist</i> instalment that month as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Smiley12ff_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smiley12ff-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The time in Hampstead was the occasion for a growing bond between Dickens and John Forster to develop and Forster soon became his unofficial business manager, and the first to read his work.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg/170px-Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg/255px-Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg/340px-Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="493" data-file-height="608" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolly_Varden_by_William_Powell_Frith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Barnaby Rudge</i> was Dickens\'s first popular failure\xe2\x80\x94but the character of Dolly Varden, "pretty, witty, sexy, became central to numerous theatrical adaptations"<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>His success as a novelist continued. The young <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> read both <i>Oliver Twist</i> and <i>Pickwick</i>, staying up until midnight to discuss them.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Nickleby" title="Nicholas Nickleby">Nicholas Nickleby</a></i> (1838\xe2\x80\x9339), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Curiosity_Shop" title="The Old Curiosity Shop">The Old Curiosity Shop</a></i> (1840\xe2\x80\x9341) and, finally, his first historical novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Barnaby_Rudge" title="Barnaby Rudge">Barnaby Rudge: A Tale of the Riots of \'Eighty</a></i>, as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Master_Humphrey%27s_Clock" title="Master Humphrey&#39;s Clock">Master Humphrey\'s Clock</a></i> series (1840\xe2\x80\x9341), were all published in monthly instalments before being made into books.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the midst of all his activity during this period there was discontent with his publishers and John Macrone was bought off, while <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bentley_(publisher)" title="Richard Bentley (publisher)">Richard Bentley</a> signed over all his rights in <i>Oliver Twist</i>. Other signs of a certain restlessness and discontent emerge\xe2\x80\x94in <a href="/wiki/Broadstairs" title="Broadstairs">Broadstairs</a> he flirted with Eleanor Picken, the young fianc\xc3\xa9e of his solicitor\'s best friend, and one night grabbed her and ran with her down to the sea. He declared they were both to drown there in the "sad sea waves". She finally got free but afterwards kept her distance. In June 1841 he precipitously set out on a two-month tour of Scotland and then, in September 1841, telegraphed Forster that he had decided to go to America.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <i>Master Humphrey\'s Clock</i> was shut down, though Dickens was still keen on the idea of the weekly magazine, a form he liked, a liking that had begun with his childhood reading of the eighteenth-century magazines <i><a href="/wiki/Tatler_(1709_journal)" title="Tatler (1709 journal)">Tatler</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i>.\n</p><p>Dickens was perturbed by the return to power of the Tories, whom Dickens described as "people whom, politically, I despise and abhor."<sup id="cite_ref-Slater_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slater-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> He had been tempted to stand for the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberals</a> in Reading, but decided against it due to financial straits.<sup id="cite_ref-Slater_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slater-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> He wrote three anti-Tory verse satires ("The Fine Old English Gentleman", "The Quack Doctor\'s Proclamation", and "Subjects for Painters") which were published in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Examiner_(1808%E2%80%9386)" title="The Examiner (1808\xe2\x80\x9386)">The Examiner</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_visit_to_the_United_States">First visit to the United States</span></h2>\n<p>In 1842, Dickens and his wife made their first trip to the United States and Canada. At this time <a href="/wiki/Georgina_Hogarth" title="Georgina Hogarth">Georgina Hogarth</a>, another sister of Catherine, joined the Dickens household, now living at Devonshire Terrace, <a href="/wiki/Marylebone" title="Marylebone">Marylebone</a>, to care for the young family they had left behind.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> She remained with them as housekeeper, organiser, adviser, and friend until Dickens\'s death in 1870.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith10ff_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith10ff-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Dickens modeled the character of <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Wickfield" title="Agnes Wickfield">Agnes Wickfield</a> after Georgina and Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg/170px-Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg/255px-Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg/340px-Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2951" data-file-height="4056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_sketch_1842.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sketch of Dickens in 1842 during his first American tour. Sketch of Dickens\'s sister Fanny, bottom left</div></div></div>\n<p>He described his impressions in a <a href="/wiki/Travel_literature" title="Travel literature">travelogue</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/American_Notes" title="American Notes">American Notes for General Circulation</a></i>. Dickens includes in <i>Notes</i> a powerful condemnation of slavery, which he had attacked as early as <i>The Pickwick Papers</i>, correlating the emancipation of the poor in England with the abolition of slavery abroad<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> citing newspaper accounts of runaway slaves disfigured by their masters. In spite of the abolitionist sentiments gleaned from his trip to America, some modern commentators have pointed out inconsistencies in Dickens\'s views on racial inequality, for instance, he has been criticized for his subsequent acquiescence in Governor <a href="/wiki/Edward_John_Eyre" title="Edward John Eyre">Eyre\'s</a> harsh crackdown during the 1860s <a href="/wiki/Morant_Bay_rebellion" title="Morant Bay rebellion">Morant Bay rebellion</a> in Jamaica and his failure to join other British progressives in condemning it.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> From <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a>, Dickens returned to Washington, D.C., and started a trek westward to St. Louis, Missouri. While there, he expressed a desire to see an American prairie before returning east. A group of 13 men then set out with Dickens to visit Looking Glass Prairie, a trip 30 miles into <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>.\n</p><p>During his American visit, Dickens spent a month in New York City, giving lectures, raising <a href="/wiki/History_of_copyright_law" title="History of copyright law">the question of international copyright laws</a> and the pirating of his work in America.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> He persuaded a group of twenty-five writers, headed by <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a>, to sign a petition for him to take to Congress, but the press were generally hostile to this, saying that he should be grateful for his popularity and that it was mercenary to complain about his work being pirated.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The popularity he gained caused a shift in his self-perception according to critic Kate Flint, who writes that he "found himself a cultural commodity, and its circulation had passed out his control", causing him to become interested in and delve into themes of public and personal personas in the next novels.<sup id="cite_ref-flint35_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flint35-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> She writes that he assumed a role of "influential commentator", publicly and in his fiction, evident in his next few books.<sup id="cite_ref-flint35_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flint35-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> His trip to the U.S. ended with a trip to Canada: Niagara Falls, Toronto, Kingston and Montreal where he appeared on stage in light comedies.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Soon after his return to England, Dickens began work on the first of his Christmas stories, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Christmas_Carol" title="A Christmas Carol">A Christmas Carol</a></i>, written in 1843, which was followed by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chimes" title="The Chimes">The Chimes</a></i> in 1844 and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cricket_on_the_Hearth" title="The Cricket on the Hearth">The Cricket on the Hearth</a></i> in 1845. Of these, <i>A Christmas Carol</i> was most popular and, tapping into an old tradition, did much to promote a renewed enthusiasm for the joys of Christmas in Britain and America.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The seeds for the story became planted in Dickens\'s mind during a trip to Manchester to witness the conditions of the manufacturing workers there. This, along with scenes he had recently witnessed at the Field Lane <a href="/wiki/Ragged_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Ragged School">Ragged School</a>, caused Dickens to resolve to "strike a sledge hammer blow" for the poor. As the idea for the story took shape and the writing began in earnest, Dickens became engrossed in the book. He later wrote that as the tale unfolded he "wept and laughed, and wept again" as he "walked about the black streets of London fifteen or twenty miles many a night when all sober folks had gone to bed."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After living briefly in Italy (1844), Dickens travelled to Switzerland (1846), where he began work on <i><a href="/wiki/Dombey_and_Son" title="Dombey and Son">Dombey and Son</a></i> (1846\xe2\x80\x9348). This and <i><a href="/wiki/David_Copperfield" title="David Copperfield">David Copperfield</a></i> (1849\xe2\x80\x9350) mark a significant artistic break in Dickens\'s career as his novels became more serious in theme and more carefully planned than his early works.\n</p><p>At about this time, he was made aware of a large embezzlement at the firm where his brother, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Dickens" title="Augustus Dickens">Augustus</a>, worked (John Chapman &amp; Co.). It had been carried out by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Powell_(1809-1887)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Powell (1809-1887)">Thomas Powell</a>, a clerk, who was on friendly terms with Dickens and who had acted as mentor to Augustus when he started work. Powell was also an author and poet and knew many of the famous writers of the day. After further fraudulent activities, Powell fled to New York and published a book called <i>The Living Authors of England</i> with a chapter on Charles Dickens, who was not amused by what Powell had written. One item that seemed to have annoyed him was the assertion that he had based the character of Paul Dombey (<i><a href="/wiki/Dombey_and_Son" title="Dombey and Son">Dombey and Son</a></i>) on Thomas Chapman, one of the principal partners at John Chapman &amp; Co. Dickens immediately sent a letter to <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Gaylord_Clark" title="Lewis Gaylord Clark">Lewis Gaylord Clark</a>, editor of the New York literary magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_Knickerbocker" title="The Knickerbocker">The Knickerbocker</a></i>, saying that Powell was a forger and thief. Clark published the letter in the <i><a href="/wiki/New-York_Tribune" title="New-York Tribune">New-York Tribune</a></i>, and several other papers picked up on the story. Powell began proceedings to sue these publications, and Clark was arrested. Dickens, realising that he had acted in haste, contacted John Chapman &amp; Co. to seek written confirmation of Powell\'s guilt. Dickens did receive a reply confirming Powell\'s embezzlement, but once the directors realised this information might have to be produced in court, they refused to make further disclosures. Owing to the difficulties of providing evidence in America to support his accusations, Dickens eventually made a private settlement with Powell out of court.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philanthropy">Philanthropy</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_(4671094).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_%284671094%29.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_%284671094%29.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_%284671094%29.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_%284671094%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_%284671094%29.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_%284671094%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="967" data-file-height="1332" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Charles_Dickens_(4671094).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Charles Dickens c.1850</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Angela_Burdett-Coutts,_1st_Baroness_Burdett-Coutts" title="Angela Burdett-Coutts, 1st Baroness Burdett-Coutts">Angela Burdett Coutts</a>, heir to the Coutts banking fortune, approached Dickens in May 1846 about setting up a home for the redemption of <a href="/wiki/Fallen_woman" title="Fallen woman">fallen women</a> of the working class. Coutts envisioned a home that would replace the punitive regimes of existing institutions with a reformative environment conducive to education and proficiency in domestic household chores. After initially resisting, Dickens eventually founded the home, named "Urania Cottage", in the Lime Grove section of <a href="/wiki/Shepherds_Bush" class="mw-redirect" title="Shepherds Bush">Shepherds Bush</a>, which he managed for ten years,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> setting the house rules, reviewing the accounts and interviewing prospective residents.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Emigration and marriage were central to Dickens\'s agenda for the women on leaving Urania Cottage, from which it is estimated that about 100 women graduated between 1847 and 1859.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span></h2>\n<p>As a young man Dickens expressed a distaste for certain aspects of organized religion. In 1836, in a pamphlet titled <i>Sunday Under Three Heads</i>, he defended the people\'s right to pleasure, opposing a plan to prohibit games on Sundays. "Look into your churches- diminished congregations and scanty attendance. People have grown sullen and obstinate, and are becoming disgusted with the faith which condemns them to such a day as this, once in every seven. They display their feeling by staying away [from church]. Turn into the streets [on a Sunday] and mark the rigid gloom that reigns over everything around"<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens honoured the figure of <span class="nowrap">Christ\xe2\x80\x94</span>though some claim he may have denied his divinity.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Notwithstanding, Dickens has been characterized as a professing Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> His son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding_Dickens" title="Henry Fielding Dickens">Henry Fielding Dickens</a>, described Dickens as someone who "possessed deep religious convictions". In the early 1840s Dickens had shown an interest in <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Browning" title="Robert Browning">Robert Browning</a> remarked that \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Dickens is an enlightened Unitarian.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Writer Gary Colledge, however, asserted that he "never strayed from his attachment to popular lay <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> He also wrote a religious work called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_of_Our_Lord" title="The Life of Our Lord">The Life of Our Lord</a></i> (1849), which was a short book about the life of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>, written with the purpose of inculcating his faith to his children and family.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens disapproved of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a> and 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelicalism</a>, and was critical of what he saw as the hypocrisy of religious institutions and philosophies like <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">spiritualism</a>, all of which he considered deviations from the true spirit of Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a> referred to Dickens as "that great Christian writer".<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_years">Middle years</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg/170px-Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg" width="170" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg/255px-Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg/340px-Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1336" data-file-height="1219" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Little_Dorrit_avatar_1856.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Little Dorrit" 1856</div></div></div>\n<p>In December 1845, Dickens took up the editorship of the London-based <i>Daily News</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> paper through which Dickens hoped to advocate, in his own words, "the Principles of Progress and Improvement, of Education and Civil and Religious Liberty and Equal Legislation."<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Among the other contributors Dickens chose to write for the paper were the radical economist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hodgskin" title="Thomas Hodgskin">Thomas Hodgskin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Dickens lasted only ten weeks on the job before resigning due to a combination of exhaustion and frustration with one of the paper\'s co-owners.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Francophile Dickens often holidayed in France, and in a speech delivered in Paris in 1846 in French called the French "the first people in the universe".<sup id="cite_ref-Soubigou_pages_154-167_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soubigou_pages_154-167-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> During his visit to Paris, Dickens met the French literati Alexandre Dumas, Victor Hugo, Eug\xc3\xa8ne Scribe, Th\xc3\xa9ophile Gautier, Fran\xc3\xa7ois-Ren\xc3\xa9 de Chateaubriand and Eug\xc3\xa8ne Sue.<sup id="cite_ref-Soubigou_pages_154-167_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soubigou_pages_154-167-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Dickens started to write <i><a href="/wiki/David_Copperfield" title="David Copperfield">David Copperfield</a></i> in 1848. It was published between 1849 and 1850. Scholars consider it as Dickens\'s veiled autobiography with the title character modeled after the author himself. It was Dickens\'s personal favourite among his own novels.\n</p><p>In late November 1851, Dickens moved into <a href="/wiki/Tavistock_House" title="Tavistock House">Tavistock House</a> where he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Bleak_House" title="Bleak House">Bleak House</a></i> (1852\xe2\x80\x9353), <i><a href="/wiki/Hard_Times_(novel)" title="Hard Times (novel)">Hard Times</a></i> (1854), and <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Dorrit" title="Little Dorrit">Little Dorrit</a></i> (1856).<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> It was here that he indulged in the amateur theatricals described in Forster\'s <i>Life</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> During this period he worked closely with the novelist and playwright <a href="/wiki/Wilkie_Collins" title="Wilkie Collins">Wilkie Collins</a>. In 1856, his income from writing allowed him to buy <a href="/wiki/Gad%27s_Hill_Place" class="mw-redirect" title="Gad&#39;s Hill Place">Gad\'s Hill Place</a> in <a href="/wiki/Higham,_Kent" title="Higham, Kent">Higham, Kent</a>. As a child, Dickens had walked past the house and dreamed of living in it. The area was also the scene of some of the events of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare\'s</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_1" title="Henry IV, Part 1">Henry IV, Part 1</a></i>, and this literary connection pleased him.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Ternan.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Ellen_Ternan.jpeg/170px-Ellen_Ternan.jpeg" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Ellen_Ternan.jpeg/255px-Ellen_Ternan.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Ellen_Ternan.jpeg/340px-Ellen_Ternan.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="835" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Ternan.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ellen_Ternan" title="Ellen Ternan">Ellen Ternan</a>, 1858</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1857, Dickens hired professional actresses for the play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Frozen_Deep" title="The Frozen Deep">The Frozen Deep</a></i>, written by him and his <a href="/wiki/Mentorship" title="Mentorship">prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilkie_Collins" title="Wilkie Collins">Wilkie Collins</a>. Dickens fell in love with one of the actresses, <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Ternan" title="Ellen Ternan">Ellen Ternan</a>, and this passion was to last the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Dickens was 45 and Ternan 18 when he made the decision, which went strongly against Victorian convention, to separate from his wife, Catherine, in 1858\xe2\x80\x94divorce was still unthinkable for someone as famous as he was. When Catherine left, never to see her husband again, she took with her one child, leaving the other children to be raised by her sister Georgina who chose to stay at Gad\'s Hill.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith10ff_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith10ff-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During this period, whilst pondering a project to give public readings for his own profit, Dickens was approached through a charitable appeal by <a href="/wiki/Great_Ormond_Street_Hospital" title="Great Ormond Street Hospital">Great Ormond Street Hospital</a>, to help it survive its first major financial crisis. His \'Drooping Buds\' essay in <i><a href="/wiki/Household_Words" title="Household Words">Household Words</a></i> earlier on 3 April 1852 was considered by the hospital\'s founders to have been the catalyst for the hospital\'s success.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Dickens, whose philanthropy was well-known, was asked by his friend, the hospital\'s founder <a href="/wiki/Charles_West_(physician)" title="Charles West (physician)">Charles West</a>, to preside over the appeal, and he threw himself into the task, heart and soul.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s public readings secured sufficient funds for an endowment to put the hospital on a sound financial footing\xe2\x80\x94one reading on 9 February 1858 alone raised \xc2\xa33,000.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After separating from Catherine,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Dickens undertook a series of hugely popular and remunerative reading tours which, together with his journalism, were to absorb most of his creative energies for the next decade, in which he was to write only two more novels.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> His first reading tour, lasting from April 1858 to February 1859, consisted of 129 appearances in 49 different towns throughout England, Scotland and Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s continued fascination with the theatrical world was written into the theatre scenes in <i>Nicholas Nickleby</i>, but more importantly he found an outlet in public readings. In 1866, he undertook a series of public readings in England and Scotland, with more the following year in England and Ireland.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png/170px-Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png/255px-Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png/340px-Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png 2x" data-file-width="626" data-file-height="786" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens_by_Watkins_1858.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dickens at his desk, 1858</div></div></div>\n<p>Major works soon followed, including <i><a href="/wiki/A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="A Tale of Two Cities">A Tale of Two Cities</a></i> (1859) and <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations">Great Expectations</a></i> (1861), which were resounding successes. During this time he was also the publisher, editor, and a major contributor to the journals <i>Household Words</i> (1850\xe2\x80\x931859) and <i><a href="/wiki/All_the_Year_Round" title="All the Year Round">All the Year Round</a></i> (1858\xe2\x80\x931870).<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early September 1860, in a field behind Gad\'s Hill, Dickens made a bonfire of most of his correspondence\xe2\x80\x94only those letters on business matters were spared. Since Ellen Ternan also destroyed all of his letters to her,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> the extent of the affair between the two remains speculative.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In the 1930s, Thomas Wright recounted that Ternan had unburdened herself to a Canon Benham, and gave currency to rumours they had been lovers.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> That the two had a son who died in infancy was alleged by Dickens\'s daughter, Kate Perugini, whom Gladys Storey had interviewed before her death in 1929. Storey published her account in <i>Dickens and Daughter</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> but no contemporary evidence exists. On his death, Dickens settled an <a href="/wiki/Life_annuity" title="Life annuity">annuity</a> on Ternan which made her a financially independent woman. <a href="/wiki/Claire_Tomalin" title="Claire Tomalin">Claire Tomalin</a>\'s book, <i>The Invisible Woman</i>, argues that Ternan lived with Dickens secretly for the last 13 years of his life. The book was subsequently turned into a play, <i>Little Nell</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Gray" title="Simon Gray">Simon Gray</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Invisible_Woman_(2013_film)" title="The Invisible Woman (2013 film)">a 2013 film</a>. In the same period, Dickens furthered his interest in the <a href="/wiki/Paranormal" title="Paranormal">paranormal</a>, becoming one of the early members of <a href="/wiki/The_Ghost_Club" title="The Ghost Club">The Ghost Club</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 1862, he was offered \xc2\xa310,000 for a reading tour of Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> He was enthusiastic, and even planned a travel book, <i>The Uncommercial Traveller Upside Down</i>, but ultimately decided against the tour.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Two of his sons, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_D%27Orsay_Tennyson_Dickens" title="Alfred D&#39;Orsay Tennyson Dickens">Alfred D\'Orsay Tennyson Dickens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Dickens" title="Edward Dickens">Edward Bulwer Lytton Dickens</a>, migrated to Australia, Edward becoming a member of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_New_South_Wales" title="Parliament of New South Wales">Parliament of New South Wales</a> as <a href="/wiki/Electoral_district_of_Wilcannia" title="Electoral district of Wilcannia">Member for Wilcannia</a> between 1889 and 1894.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg/220px-Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg/330px-Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg/440px-Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1898" data-file-height="1336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Staplehurst_rail_crash.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After the <a href="/wiki/Staplehurst_rail_crash" title="Staplehurst rail crash">Staplehurst rail crash</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On 9 June 1865, while returning from Paris with Ellen Ternan, Dickens was involved in the <a href="/wiki/Staplehurst_rail_crash" title="Staplehurst rail crash">Staplehurst rail crash</a>. The train\'s first seven carriages plunged off a <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> bridge that was under repair. The only <a href="/wiki/First_class_travel" title="First class travel">first-class</a> carriage to remain on the track was the one in which Dickens was travelling. Before rescuers arrived, Dickens tended and comforted the wounded and the dying with a flask of brandy and a hat refreshed with water, and saved some lives. Before leaving, he remembered the unfinished manuscript for <i><a href="/wiki/Our_Mutual_Friend" title="Our Mutual Friend">Our Mutual Friend</a></i>, and he returned to his carriage to retrieve it.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Dickens later used this experience as material for his short <a href="/wiki/Ghost_story" title="Ghost story">ghost story</a>, "<a href="/wiki/The_Signal-Man" title="The Signal-Man">The Signal-Man</a>", in which the central character has a premonition of his own death in a rail crash. He also based the story on several previous <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_rail_accidents" title="Lists of rail accidents">rail accidents</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Clayton_Tunnel_rail_crash" title="Clayton Tunnel rail crash">Clayton Tunnel rail crash</a> of 1861. Dickens managed to avoid an appearance at the <a href="/wiki/Inquest" title="Inquest">inquest</a> to avoid disclosing that he had been travelling with Ternan and her mother, which would have caused a scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_visit_to_the_United_States">Second visit to the United States</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall,_New_York,_New_York,_1867.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall%2C_New_York%2C_New_York%2C_1867.jpg/220px-Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall%2C_New_York%2C_New_York%2C_1867.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall%2C_New_York%2C_New_York%2C_1867.jpg/330px-Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall%2C_New_York%2C_New_York%2C_1867.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall%2C_New_York%2C_New_York%2C_1867.jpg/440px-Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall%2C_New_York%2C_New_York%2C_1867.jpg 2x" data-file-width="890" data-file-height="673" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buying_tickets_for_a_Charles_Dickens_reading_at_Steinway_Hall,_New_York,_New_York,_1867.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crowd of spectators buying tickets for a Dickens reading at <a href="/wiki/Steinway_Hall" title="Steinway Hall">Steinway Hall</a>, New York City in 1867</div></div></div>\n<p>While he contemplated a second visit to the United States, the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> in America in 1861 delayed his plans. On 9 November 1867, over two years after the war, Dickens set sail from <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> for his second American reading tour. Landing at <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, he devoted the rest of the month to a round of dinners with such notables as <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wadsworth_Longfellow" title="Henry Wadsworth Longfellow">Henry Wadsworth Longfellow</a>, and his American publisher, <a href="/wiki/James_Thomas_Fields" title="James Thomas Fields">James Thomas Fields</a>. In early December, the readings began. He performed 76 readings, netting \xc2\xa319,000, from December 1867 to April 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-Hobsbaum1998_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hobsbaum1998-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Dickens shuttled between Boston and New York, where he gave 22 readings at <a href="/wiki/Steinway_Hall" title="Steinway Hall">Steinway Hall</a>. Although he had started to suffer from what he called the "true American <a href="/wiki/Catarrh" title="Catarrh">catarrh</a>", he kept to a schedule that would have challenged a much younger man, even managing to squeeze in some sleighing in <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg/170px-Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg/255px-Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg/340px-Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2312" data-file-height="3468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickensposter_nottingham1869.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster promoting a reading by Dickens in <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a> dated 4 February 1869, two months before he suffered a mild stroke</div></div></div>\n<p>During his travels, he saw a change in the people and the circumstances of America. His final appearance was at a banquet the American Press held in his honour at <a href="/wiki/Delmonico%27s_Restaurant" class="mw-redirect" title="Delmonico&#39;s Restaurant">Delmonico\'s</a> on 18 April, when he promised never to denounce America again.
