By the end of the tour Dickens could hardly manage solid food, subsisting on champagne and eggs beaten in sherry. On 23 April he boarded the <a href="/wiki/Cunard" class="mw-redirect" title="Cunard">Cunard</a> liner <a href="/wiki/SS_Russia_(1867)" title="SS Russia (1867)"><i>Russia</i></a> to return to Britain,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> barely escaping a <a href="/wiki/Tax_lien" title="Tax lien">Federal Tax Lien</a> against the proceeds of his lecture tour.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Farewell_readings">Farewell readings</span></h2>\n<p>Between 1868 and 1869, Dickens gave a series of "farewell readings" in England, Scotland, and Ireland, beginning on 6 October. He managed, of a contracted 100 readings, to deliver 75 in the provinces, with a further 12 in London.<sup id="cite_ref-Hobsbaum1998_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hobsbaum1998-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> As he pressed on he was affected by giddiness and fits of paralysis. He suffered a stroke on 18 April 1869 in Chester.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> He collapsed on 22 April 1869, at <a href="/wiki/Preston,_Lancashire" title="Preston, Lancashire">Preston</a> in Lancashire, and on doctor\'s advice, the tour was cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> After further provincial readings were cancelled, he began work on his final novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mystery_of_Edwin_Drood" title="The Mystery of Edwin Drood">The Mystery of Edwin Drood</a></i>. It was fashionable in the 1860s to \'do the slums\' and, in company, Dickens visited <a href="/wiki/Opium_den" title="Opium den">opium dens</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shadwell" title="Shadwell">Shadwell</a>, where he witnessed an elderly addict known as "<a href="/wiki/Lascar" title="Lascar">Laskar</a> Sal", who formed the model for the "Opium Sal" subsequently featured in his mystery novel, <i>Edwin Drood</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Dickens had regained sufficient strength, he arranged, with medical approval, for a final series of readings to partially make up to his sponsors what they had lost due to his illness. There were to be 12 performances, running between 11 January and 15 March 1870, the last at 8:00&#160;pm at <a href="/wiki/St._James%27s_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="St. James&#39;s Hall">St. James\'s Hall</a> in London. Although in grave health by this time, he read <i>A Christmas Carol</i> and <i>The Trial from Pickwick</i>. On 2 May, he made his last public appearance at a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> Banquet in the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">Prince</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_of_Denmark" title="Alexandra of Denmark">Princess of Wales</a>, paying a special tribute on the death of his friend, the illustrator Daniel Maclise.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_(The_Graphic,_1870).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_%28The_Graphic%2C_1870%29.jpg/220px-Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_%28The_Graphic%2C_1870%29.jpg" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_%28The_Graphic%2C_1870%29.jpg/330px-Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_%28The_Graphic%2C_1870%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_%28The_Graphic%2C_1870%29.jpg/440px-Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_%28The_Graphic%2C_1870%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="711" data-file-height="424" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Samuel_Luke_Fildes_-_The_Empty_Chair_(The_Graphic,_1870).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Luke_Fildes" title="Luke Fildes">Samuel Luke Fildes</a>\xe2\x80\x94<i>The Empty Chair</i>. Fildes was illustrating <i>Edwin Drood</i> at the time of Charles Dickens\'s death. The engraving shows Dickens\'s empty chair in his study at <a href="/wiki/Gads_Hill_Place" title="Gads Hill Place">Gads Hill Place</a>. It appeared in the Christmas 1870 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graphic" title="The Graphic">The Graphic</a></i> and thousands of prints of it were sold.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg/220px-Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg/330px-Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg/440px-Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2601" data-file-height="1392" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_Death_Certificate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Death certificate of Charles Dickens.</div></div></div>\n<p>On 8 June 1870, Dickens suffered another stroke at his home after a full day\'s work on <i>Edwin Drood</i>. He never regained consciousness, and the next day, five years to the day after the Staplehurst rail crash, he died at Gads Hill Place. Biographer Claire Tomalin has suggested Dickens was actually in Peckham when he suffered the stroke, and his mistress Ellen Ternan and her maids had him taken back to Gad\'s Hill so the public would not know the truth about their relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Contrary to his wish to be buried at <a href="/wiki/Rochester_Cathedral" title="Rochester Cathedral">Rochester Cathedral</a> "in an inexpensive, unostentatious, and strictly private manner",<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> he was laid to rest in the <a href="/wiki/Poets%27_Corner" title="Poets&#39; Corner">Poets\' Corner</a> of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>. A printed epitaph circulated at the time of the funeral reads:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To the Memory of Charles Dickens (England\'s most popular author) who died at his residence, Higham, near Rochester, Kent, 9&#160;June 1870, aged 58 years. He was a sympathiser with the poor, the suffering, and the oppressed; and by his death, one of England\'s greatest writers is lost to the world.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>His last words were: "On the ground", in response to his sister-in-law Georgina\'s request that he lie down.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> On Sunday, 19 June 1870, five days after Dickens was buried in the Abbey, Dean <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Penrhyn_Stanley" title="Arthur Penrhyn Stanley">Arthur Penrhyn Stanley</a> delivered a memorial elegy, lauding "the genial and loving humorist whom we now mourn", for showing by his own example "that even in dealing with the darkest scenes and the most degraded characters, genius could still be clean, and mirth could be innocent". Pointing to the fresh flowers that adorned the novelist\'s grave, Stanley assured those present that "the spot would thenceforth be a sacred one with both the New World and the Old, as that of the representative of literature, not of this island only, but of all who speak our English tongue."<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his will, drafted more than a year before his death, Dickens left the care of his \xc2\xa380,000 estate to his longtime colleague John Forster and his "best and truest friend" Georgina Hogarth who, along with Dickens\'s two sons, also received a tax-free sum of \xc2\xa38,000 (about \xc2\xa3800,000 in present terms). Although Dickens and his wife had been separated for several years at the time of his death, he provided her with an annual income of \xc2\xa3600 and made her similar allowances in his will. He also bequeathed \xc2\xa319 19s to each servant in his employment at the time of his death.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_style">Literary style</span></h2>\n<p>Dickens favoured the style of the 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Picaresque_novels" class="mw-redirect" title="Picaresque novels">picaresque novels</a> that he found in abundance on his father\'s shelves. According to Ackroyd, other than these, perhaps the most important literary influence on him was derived from the fables of <i>The Arabian Nights</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens_dream.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dickens_dream.jpg/220px-Dickens_dream.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dickens_dream.jpg/330px-Dickens_dream.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dickens_dream.jpg/440px-Dickens_dream.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="462" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens_dream.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dickens\'s Dream</i> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_William_Buss" title="Robert William Buss">Robert William Buss</a>, portraying Dickens at his desk at <a href="/wiki/Gads_Hill_Place" title="Gads Hill Place">Gads Hill Place</a> surrounded by many of his characters</div></div></div>\n<p>His writing style is marked by a profuse linguistic creativity.<sup id="cite_ref-Mee2010_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mee2010-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Satire, flourishing in his gift for caricature, is his forte. An early reviewer compared him to <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">Hogarth</a> for his keen practical sense of the ludicrous side of life, though his acclaimed mastery of varieties of class idiom may in fact mirror the conventions of contemporary popular theatre.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Dickens worked intensively on developing arresting names for his characters that would reverberate with associations for his readers, and assist the development of motifs in the storyline, giving what one critic calls an "allegorical impetus" to the novels\' meanings.<sup id="cite_ref-Mee2010_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mee2010-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> To cite one of numerous examples, the name Mr. Murdstone in <i>David Copperfield</i> conjures up twin allusions to "murder" and stony coldness.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> His literary style is also a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realism</a>. His satires of British aristocratic snobbery\xe2\x80\x94he calls one character the "Noble Refrigerator"\xe2\x80\x94are often popular. Comparing orphans to stocks and shares, people to tug boats, or dinner-party guests to furniture are just some of Dickens\'s acclaimed flights of fancy.\n</p><p>The author worked closely with his illustrators, supplying them with a summary of the work at the outset and thus ensuring that his characters and settings were exactly how he envisioned them. He briefed the illustrator on plans for each month\'s instalment so that work could begin before he wrote them. <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Stone" title="Marcus Stone">Marcus Stone</a>, illustrator of <i>Our Mutual Friend</i>, recalled that the author was always "ready to describe down to the minutest details the personal characteristics, and&#160;... life-history of the creations of his fancy".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Characters">Characters</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg/220px-Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg/330px-Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg/440px-Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="923" data-file-height="584" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_characters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dickens\' characters</div></div></div>\n<p>Dickens\'s biographer <a href="/wiki/Claire_Tomalin" title="Claire Tomalin">Claire Tomalin</a> regards him as the greatest creator of character in English fiction after <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\nDickensian <a href="/wiki/List_of_Dickensian_characters" title="List of Dickensian characters">characters</a> are amongst the most memorable in English literature, especially so because of their typically whimsical names. The likes of <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Scrooge" title="Ebenezer Scrooge">Ebenezer Scrooge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiny_Tim_(A_Christmas_Carol)" title="Tiny Tim (A Christmas Carol)">Tiny Tim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Marley" title="Jacob Marley">Jacob Marley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Cratchit" title="Bob Cratchit">Bob Cratchit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist_(character)" title="Oliver Twist (character)">Oliver Twist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artful_Dodger" title="Artful Dodger">The Artful Dodger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fagin" title="Fagin">Fagin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Sikes" title="Bill Sikes">Bill Sikes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations">Pip</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miss_Havisham" title="Miss Havisham">Miss Havisham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Carton" title="Sydney Carton">Sydney Carton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darnay" title="Charles Darnay">Charles Darnay</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Copperfield_(character)" title="David Copperfield (character)">David Copperfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilkins_Micawber" title="Wilkins Micawber">Mr. Micawber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abel_Magwitch" title="Abel Magwitch">Abel Magwitch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quilp" title="Quilp">Daniel Quilp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pickwick" title="Samuel Pickwick">Samuel Pickwick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wackford_Squeers" class="mw-redirect" title="Wackford Squeers">Wackford Squeers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uriah_Heep" title="Uriah Heep">Uriah Heep</a> are so well known as to be part and parcel of British culture, and in some cases have passed into ordinary language: a <i>scrooge</i>, for example, is a miser - or someone who dislikes Christmas festivity.\n</p><p>His characters were often so memorable that they took on a life of their own outside his books. "Gamp" became a slang expression for an umbrella from the character <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Gamp" title="Sarah Gamp">Mrs Gamp</a>, and "Pickwickian", "Pecksniffian", and "Gradgrind" all entered dictionaries due to Dickens\'s original portraits of such characters who were, respectively, <a href="/wiki/Quixotism" title="Quixotism">quixotic</a>, hypocritical, and vapidly factual. Many were drawn from real life: Mrs Nickleby is based on his mother, though she didn\'t recognise herself in the portrait,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> just as Mr Micawber is constructed from aspects of his father\'s \'rhetorical exuberance\':<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Harold Skimpole in <i>Bleak House</i> is based on <a href="/wiki/James_Henry_Leigh_Hunt" class="mw-redirect" title="James Henry Leigh Hunt">James Henry Leigh Hunt</a>: his wife\'s dwarfish chiropodist recognised herself in Miss Mowcher in <i>David Copperfield</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps Dickens\'s impressions on his meeting with Hans Christian Andersen informed the delineation of Uriah Heep.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> maintained that "we remodel our psychological geography when we read Dickens" as he produces "characters who exist not in detail, not accurately or exactly, but abundantly in a cluster of wild yet extraordinarily revealing remarks".<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> One "character" vividly drawn throughout his novels is London itself. From the <a href="/wiki/Coaching_inn" title="Coaching inn">coaching inns</a> on the outskirts of the city to the lower reaches of the <a href="/wiki/Thames" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames">Thames</a>, all aspects of the capital are described over the course of his body of work.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autobiographical_elements">Autobiographical elements</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Copperfield,_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/David_Copperfield%2C_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg/220px-David_Copperfield%2C_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/David_Copperfield%2C_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg/330px-David_Copperfield%2C_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/David_Copperfield%2C_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg/440px-David_Copperfield%2C_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="371" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Copperfield,_We_are_disturbed_in_our_cookery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An original illustration by <a href="/wiki/Phiz" class="mw-redirect" title="Phiz">Phiz</a> from the novel <i>David Copperfield</i>, which is widely regarded as Dickens\'s most autobiographical work</div></div></div>\n<p>Authors frequently draw their portraits of characters from people they have known in real life. <i>David Copperfield</i> is regarded by many as a veiled autobiography of Dickens. The scenes of interminable court cases and legal arguments in <i>Bleak House</i> reflect Dickens\'s experiences as a law clerk and court reporter, and in particular his direct experience of the law\'s procedural delay during 1844 when he sued publishers in Chancery for breach of copyright.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s father was sent to prison for debt, and this became a common theme in many of his books, with the detailed depiction of life in the Marshalsea prison in <i>Little Dorrit</i> resulting from Dickens\'s own experiences of the institution.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Lucy Stroughill, a childhood sweetheart, may have affected several of Dickens\'s portraits of girls such as Little Em\'ly in <i>David Copperfield</i> and Lucie Manette in <i>A Tale of Two Cities.</i><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup> Dickens may have drawn on his childhood experiences, but he was also ashamed of them and would not reveal that this was where he gathered his realistic accounts of squalor. Very few knew the details of his early life until six years after his death, when John Forster published a biography on which Dickens had collaborated. Though Skimpole brutally sends up Leigh Hunt, some critics have detected in his portrait features of Dickens\'s own character, which he sought to exorcise by self-parody.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Episodic_writing">Episodic writing</span></h3>\n<p>Most of Dickens\'s major novels were first written in monthly or weekly instalments in journals such as <i>Master Humphrey\'s Clock</i> and <i>Household Words</i>, later reprinted in book form. These instalments made the stories affordable and accessible, and the series of regular <a href="/wiki/Cliffhanger" title="Cliffhanger">cliff-hangers</a> made each new episode widely anticipated. When <i>The Old Curiosity Shop</i> was being serialised, American fans waited at the docks in <a href="/wiki/New_York_harbor" class="mw-redirect" title="New York harbor">New York harbor</a>, shouting out to the crew of an incoming British ship, "Is little Nell dead?"<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s talent was to incorporate this episodic writing style but still end up with a coherent novel at the end.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens,_public_reading,_1867.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Charles_Dickens%2C_public_reading%2C_1867.jpg/220px-Charles_Dickens%2C_public_reading%2C_1867.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Charles_Dickens%2C_public_reading%2C_1867.jpg/330px-Charles_Dickens%2C_public_reading%2C_1867.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Charles_Dickens%2C_public_reading%2C_1867.jpg/440px-Charles_Dickens%2C_public_reading%2C_1867.jpg 2x" data-file-width="879" data-file-height="660" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens,_public_reading,_1867.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Charles Dickens as he appears when reading." Wood engraving from <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Weekly" title="Harper&#39;s Weekly">Harper\'s Weekly</a></i>, 7 December 1867</div></div></div>\n<p>Another important impact of Dickens\'s episodic writing style resulted from his exposure to the opinions of his readers and friends. His friend Forster had a significant hand in reviewing his drafts, an influence that went beyond matters of punctuation. He toned down melodramatic and sensationalist exaggerations, cut long passages (such as the episode of Quilp\'s drowning in <i>The Old Curiosity Shop</i>), and made suggestions about plot and character. It was he who suggested that Charley Bates should be redeemed in <i>Oliver Twist</i>. Dickens had not thought of killing Little Nell, and it was Forster who advised him to entertain this possibility as necessary to his conception of the heroine.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens\'s serialisation of his novels was not uncriticised by other authors. In <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wrecker_(Stevenson_novel)" title="The Wrecker (Stevenson novel)">The Wrecker</a></i>, there is a comment by Captain Nares, investigating an abandoned ship: "See! They were writing up the log," said Nares, pointing to the ink-bottle. "Caught napping, as usual. I wonder if there ever was a captain yet that lost a ship with his log-book up to date? He generally has about a month to fill up on a clean break, like Charles Dickens and his serial novels."\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_commentary">Social commentary</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg/170px-Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg/255px-Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg/340px-Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1816" data-file-height="2564" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nurse <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Gamp" title="Sarah Gamp">Sarah Gamp</a> (left) from <i>Martin Chuzzlewit</i> became a stereotype of untrained and incompetent nurses of the early Victorian era, before the reforms of <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Dickens\'s novels were, among other things, works of <a href="/wiki/Social_commentary" title="Social commentary">social commentary</a>. He was a fierce critic of the poverty and <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social stratification</a> of <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> society. In a New York address, he expressed his belief that "Virtue shows quite as well in rags and patches as she does in purple and fine linen".<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s second novel, <i>Oliver Twist</i> (1839), shocked readers with its images of poverty and crime: it challenged middle class polemics about criminals, making impossible any pretence to ignorance about what poverty entailed.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_techniques">Literary techniques</span></h3>\n<p>Dickens is often described as using idealised characters and highly sentimental scenes to contrast with his <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">caricatures</a> and the ugly social truths he reveals. The story of Nell Trent in <i>The Old Curiosity Shop</i> (1841) was received as extraordinarily moving by contemporary readers but viewed as ludicrously sentimental by <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>. "You would need to have a heart of stone", he declared in one of his famous witticisms, "not to laugh at the death of little Nell."<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a>, stated: "It is not the death of little Nell, but the life of little Nell, that I object to", arguing that the maudlin effect of his description of her life owed much to the gregarious nature of Dickens\'s grief, his "despotic" use of people\'s feelings to move them to tears in works like this.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The question as to whether Dickens belongs to the tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Sentimental_novel" title="Sentimental novel">sentimental novel</a> is debatable. Valerie Purton, in her recent <i>Dickens and the Sentimental Tradition</i>, sees him continuing aspects of this tradition, and argues that his "sentimental scenes and characters [are] as crucial to the overall power of the novels as his darker or comic figures and scenes", and that "<i>Dombey and Son</i> is [ ... ] Dickens\'s greatest triumph in the sentimentalist tradition".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Encyclop\xc3\xa6dia Britannica</i> online comments that, despite "patches of emotional excess", such as the reported death of Tiny Tim in <i>A Christmas Carol</i> (1843), "Dickens cannot really be termed a sentimental novelist".<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <i>Oliver Twist</i> Dickens provides readers with an idealised portrait of a boy so inherently and unrealistically good that his values are never subverted by either brutal orphanages or coerced involvement in a gang of young <a href="/wiki/Pickpocketing" title="Pickpocketing">pickpockets</a>. While later novels also centre on idealised characters (Esther Summerson in <i>Bleak House</i> and Amy Dorrit in <i>Little Dorrit</i>), this idealism serves only to highlight Dickens\'s goal of poignant social commentary. Dickens\'s fiction, reflecting what he believed to be true of his own life, makes frequent use of coincidence, either for comic effect or to emphasise the idea of providence.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> For example, Oliver Twist turns out to be the lost nephew of the upper-class family that rescues him from the dangers of the pickpocket group. Such coincidences are a staple of 18th-century picaresque novels, such as Henry Fielding\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Tom_Jones,_a_Foundling" title="The History of Tom Jones, a Foundling">Tom Jones</a>,</i> which Dickens enjoyed reading as a youth.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg/220px-Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg/330px-Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg/440px-Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2490" data-file-height="2825" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottawa_Public_Library.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dickens portrait (top left), in between Shakespeare and Tennyson, on a stained glass window at the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Public_Library" title="Ottawa Public Library">Ottawa Public Library</a>, Ottawa, Canada</div></div></div>\n<p>Dickens was the most popular novelist of his time,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> and remains one of the best-known and most-read of English authors. His works have never gone <a href="/wiki/Out-of-print_book" title="Out-of-print book">out of print</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> and have been adapted continually for the screen since the invention of cinema,<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> with at least 200 motion pictures and TV adaptations based on Dickens\'s works documented.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Many of his works were adapted for the stage during his own lifetime, and as early as 1913, a silent film of <i>The Pickwick Papers</i> was made.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> He created some of the world\'s best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among fellow writers, Dickens has been both lionised and mocked. <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>, G. K. Chesterton, and <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> praised his realism, comic voice, prose fluency, and satiric caricature, as well as his <a href="/wiki/Child_labour#Historical" title="Child labour">passionate advocacy</a> on behalf of children and the poor. French writer <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a> called Dickens his favourite writer, writing his novels "stand alone, dwarfing all others by their amazing power and felicity of expression."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Dutch painter <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a> was inspired by Dickens\'s novels in several of his paintings like <i>Vincent\'s Chair</i> and in an 1889 letter to his sister stated that reading Dickens, especially <i>A Christmas Carol</i>, was one of the things that was keeping him from committing suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Oscar Wilde generally disparaged his depiction of character, while admiring his gift for caricature.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> His late contemporary <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>, by then <a href="/wiki/Poet_laureate" title="Poet laureate">Poet laureate</a>, thought him a "very talkative, vulgar young person", adding he had not read a line of his work; Dickens in return thought Wordsworth "a dreadful Old Ass".<sup id="cite_ref-Mackenzie_1870_243_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackenzie_1870_243-166">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Henry James denied him a premier position, calling him "the greatest of superficial novelists": Dickens failed to endow his characters with psychological depth and the novels, "loose baggy monsters",<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> betrayed a "cavalier organisation".<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> had a love-hate relationship with his works, finding his novels "mesmerizing" while reproving him for his sentimentalism and a commonplace style.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a> expressed his admiration for the author, "We understand Dickens in Russia, I am convinced, almost as well as the English, perhaps even with all the nuances. It may well be that we love him no less than his compatriots do. And yet how original is Dickens, and how very English!".<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>A Christmas Carol</i> is most probably his best-known story, with frequent new adaptations. It is also the most-filmed of Dickens\'s stories, with many versions dating from the early years of cinema.<sup id="cite_ref-Callow2009_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Callow2009-171">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> According to the historian <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Hutton" title="Ronald Hutton">Ronald Hutton</a>, the current state of the observance of Christmas is largely the result of a mid-Victorian revival of the holiday spearheaded by <i>A Christmas Carol</i>. Dickens catalysed the emerging Christmas as a family-centred festival of generosity, in contrast to the dwindling community-based and church-centred observations, as new middle-class expectations arose.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Its archetypal figures (Scrooge, Tiny Tim, the Christmas ghosts) entered into Western cultural consciousness. A prominent phrase from the tale, "<a href="/wiki/Christmas_and_holiday_season#History_of_the_phrase" title="Christmas and holiday season">Merry Christmas</a>", was popularised following the appearance of the story.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The term Scrooge became a synonym for miser, and his dismissive exclamation <a href="/wiki/Humbug" title="Humbug">\'Bah! Humbug!\'</a> likewise gained currency as an idiom.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Novelist <a href="/wiki/William_Makepeace_Thackeray" title="William Makepeace Thackeray">William Makepeace Thackeray</a> called the book "a national benefit, and to every man and woman who reads it a personal kindness".<sup id="cite_ref-Callow2009_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Callow2009-171">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg/220px-Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg/330px-Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg/440px-Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1668" data-file-height="1188" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bleak-house-broadstairs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bleak_House,_Broadstairs" title="Bleak House, Broadstairs">Bleak House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Broadstairs" title="Broadstairs">Broadstairs</a>, Kent, where Dickens wrote some of his novels</div></div></div>\n<p>At a time when Britain was the major economic and political power of the world, Dickens highlighted the life of the forgotten poor and disadvantaged within society. Through his journalism he campaigned on specific issues\xe2\x80\x94such as <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a>\xe2\x80\x94but his fiction probably demonstrated its greatest prowess in changing public opinion in regard to class inequalities. He often depicted the exploitation and oppression of the poor and condemned the public officials and institutions that not only allowed such abuses to exist, but flourished as a result. His most strident indictment of this condition is in <i>Hard Times</i> (1854), Dickens\'s only novel-length treatment of the industrial working class. In this work, he uses vitriol and satire to illustrate how this marginalised social stratum was termed "Hands" by the factory owners; that is, not really "people" but rather only appendages of the machines they operated. His writings inspired others, in particular journalists and political figures, to address such problems of class oppression. For example, the prison scenes in <i>The Pickwick Papers</i> are claimed to have been influential in having the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Prison" title="Fleet Prison">Fleet Prison</a> shut down. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> asserted that Dickens "issued to the world more political and social truths than have been uttered by all the professional politicians, publicists and moralists put together".<sup id="cite_ref-KucichSadoff_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KucichSadoff-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> even remarked that <i>Great Expectations</i> was more seditious than Marx\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">Das Kapital</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-KucichSadoff_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KucichSadoff-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The exceptional popularity of Dickens\'s novels, even those with socially oppositional themes (<i>Bleak House</i>, 1853; <i>Little Dorrit</i>, 1857; <i>Our Mutual Friend</i>, 1865), not only underscored his almost preternatural ability to create compelling storylines and unforgettable characters, but also ensured that the Victorian public confronted issues of social justice that had commonly been ignored. It has been argued that his technique of flooding his narratives with an \'unruly superfluity of material\' that, in the gradual d\xc3\xa9nouement, yields up an unsuspected order, influenced the organisation of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_and_legacy">Influence and legacy</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG/170px-Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG/255px-Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG/340px-Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2812" data-file-height="3750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dickens_and_Nell_Philly.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dickens_and_Little_Nell_(Elwell)" title="Dickens and Little Nell (Elwell)"><i>Dickens and Little Nell</i></a> statue in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, Pennsylvania</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg/170px-Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg/255px-Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg/340px-Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2989" data-file-height="2984" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_grave_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dickens\' grave in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> in 2012</div></div></div>\n<p>Museums and festivals celebrating Dickens\'s life and works exist in many places with which Dickens was associated. These include the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens_Museum" title="Charles Dickens Museum">Charles Dickens Museum</a> in London, the historic home where he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist" title="Oliver Twist">Oliver Twist</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pickwick_Papers" title="The Pickwick Papers">The Pickwick Papers</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Nickleby" title="Nicholas Nickleby">Nicholas Nickleby</a></i>; and the Charles Dickens Birthplace Museum in Portsmouth, the house in which he was born. The original manuscripts of many of his novels, as well as printers\' proofs, first editions, and illustrations from the collection of Dickens\'s friend John Forster are held at the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Albert_Museum" title="Victoria and Albert Museum">Victoria and Albert Museum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Dickens\'s will stipulated that no memorial be erected in his honour; nonetheless, a life-size bronze statue of Dickens entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Dickens_and_Little_Nell_(Elwell)" title="Dickens and Little Nell (Elwell)">Dickens and Little Nell</a></i>, cast in 1891 by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Edwin_Elwell" title="Francis Edwin Elwell">Francis Edwin Elwell</a>, stands in <a href="/wiki/Clark_Park" title="Clark Park">Clark Park</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Spruce_Hill,_Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect" title="Spruce Hill, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania">Spruce Hill</a> neighbourhood of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, Pennsylvania. Another life-size statue of Dickens is located at <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Park,_New_South_Wales" title="Centennial Park, New South Wales">Centennial Park</a>, Sydney, Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, a life-size statue was unveiled near his birthplace in Portsmouth on the 202nd anniversary of his birth; this was supported by the author\'s great-great grandsons, Ian and <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Charles_Dickens_(actor)" title="Gerald Charles Dickens (actor)">Gerald Dickens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens was commemorated on the <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">Series E \xc2\xa310 note</a> issued by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England_note_issues" title="Bank of England note issues">Bank of England</a> that circulated between 1992 and 2003. His portrait appeared on the reverse of the note accompanied by a scene from <i>The Pickwick Papers</i>. <a href="/wiki/The_Charles_Dickens_School" title="The Charles Dickens School">The Charles Dickens School</a> is a high school in Broadstairs, Kent. A theme park, <a href="/wiki/Dickens_World" title="Dickens World">Dickens World</a>, standing in part on the site of the former <a href="/wiki/Chatham_Dockyard" title="Chatham Dockyard">naval dockyard</a> where Dickens\'s father once worked in the Navy Pay Office, opened in <a href="/wiki/Chatham,_Kent" title="Chatham, Kent">Chatham</a> in 2007. To celebrate the 200th anniversary of the birth of Charles Dickens in 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_London" title="Museum of London">Museum of London</a> held the UK\'s first major exhibition on the author in 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, Dickens was number 41 in the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>\'s poll of the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> American literary critic <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bloom" title="Harold Bloom">Harold Bloom</a> placed Dickens among the <a href="/wiki/The_Western_Canon:_The_Books_and_School_of_the_Ages" title="The Western Canon: The Books and School of the Ages">greatest Western Writers of all time</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In the UK survey <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Read" title="The Big Read">The Big Read</a>, carried out by the BBC in 2003, five of Dickens\'s books were named in the <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Read#Top_200_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="The Big Read">Top 100</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dickens and his publications have appeared on a number of postage stamps including: UK (1970, 1993, 2011 and 2012), Soviet Union (1962), Antigua, Barbuda, Botswana, Cameroon, Dubai, Fujairah, St Christopher, Nevis and Anguilla, St Helena, St Lucia and Turks and Caicos Islands (1970), St Vincent (1987), Nevis (2007), <a href="/wiki/List_of_postage_stamps_of_Alderney" title="List of postage stamps of Alderney">Alderney</a>, Gibraltar, Jersey and Pitcairn Islands (2012), Austria (2013), Mozambique (2014).<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_works">Notable works</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens_bibliography" title="Charles Dickens bibliography">Charles Dickens bibliography</a></div>\n<p>Dickens published well over a dozen major novels and novellas, a large number of short stories, including a number of Christmas-themed stories, a handful of plays, and several non-fiction books. Dickens\'s novels were initially serialised in weekly and monthly magazines, then reprinted in standard book formats.\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Pickwick_Papers" title="The Pickwick Papers">The Pickwick Papers</a></i> (<i>The Posthumous Papers of the Pickwick Club</i>; monthly serial, April 1836 to November 1837)<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist" title="Oliver Twist">Oliver Twist</a></i> (<i>The Adventures of Oliver Twist</i>; monthly serial in <a href="/wiki/Bentley%27s_Miscellany" title="Bentley&#39;s Miscellany">Bentley\'s Miscellany</a>, February 1837 to April 1839)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Nickleby" title="Nicholas Nickleby">Nicholas Nickleby</a></i> (<i>The Life and Adventures of Nicholas Nickleby</i>; monthly serial, April 1838 to October 1839)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Curiosity_Shop" title="The Old Curiosity Shop">The Old Curiosity Shop</a></i> (weekly serial in <a href="/wiki/Master_Humphrey%27s_Clock" title="Master Humphrey&#39;s Clock">Master Humphrey\'s Clock</a>, April 1840 to November 1841)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Barnaby_Rudge" title="Barnaby Rudge">Barnaby Rudge</a></i> (<i>Barnaby Rudge: A Tale of the Riots of Eighty</i>; weekly serial in <a href="/wiki/Master_Humphrey%27s_Clock" title="Master Humphrey&#39;s Clock">Master Humphrey\'s Clock</a>, February to November 1841)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/A_Christmas_Carol" title="A Christmas Carol">A Christmas Carol</a></i> (<i>A Christmas Carol in Prose: Being a Ghost-story of Christmas</i>; 1843)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Martin_Chuzzlewit" title="Martin Chuzzlewit">Martin Chuzzlewit</a></i> (<i>The Life and Adventures of Martin Chuzzlewit</i>; monthly serial, January 1843 to July 1844)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Chimes" title="The Chimes">The Chimes</a></i> (<i>The Chimes: A Goblin Story of Some Bells That Rang an Old Year Out and a New Year In</i>; 1844)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Cricket_on_the_Hearth" title="The Cricket on the Hearth">The Cricket on the Hearth</a></i> (<i>The Cricket on the Hearth: A Fairy Tale of Home</i>; 1845)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dombey_and_Son" title="Dombey and Son">Dombey and Son</a></i> (<i>Dealings with the Firm of Dombey and Son: Wholesale, Retail and for Exportation</i>; monthly serial, October 1846 to April 1848)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Haunted_Man_and_the_Ghost%27s_Bargain" title="The Haunted Man and the Ghost&#39;s Bargain">The Haunted Man</a></i> (<i>The Haunted Man and the Ghost\'s Bargain: A Fancy for Christmas-time</i>; 1848)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/David_Copperfield" title="David Copperfield">David Copperfield</a></i> (<i>The Personal History, Adventures, Experience and Observation of David Copperfield the Younger of Blunderstone Rookery [Which He Never Meant to Publish on Any Account]</i>; monthly serial, May 1849 to November 1850)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bleak_House" title="Bleak House">Bleak House</a></i> (monthly serial, March 1852 to September 1853)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hard_Times_(novel)" title="Hard Times (novel)">Hard Times</a></i> (<i>Hard Times: For These Times</i>; weekly serial in <a href="/wiki/Household_Words" title="Household Words">Household Words</a>, 1 April 1854, to 12 August 1854)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Little_Dorrit" title="Little Dorrit">Little Dorrit</a></i> (monthly serial, December 1855 to June 1857)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="A Tale of Two Cities">A Tale of Two Cities</a></i> (weekly serial in <a href="/wiki/All_the_Year_Round" title="All the Year Round">All the Year Round</a>, 30 April 1859, to 26 November 1859)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations">Great Expectations</a></i> (weekly serial in <a href="/wiki/All_the_Year_Round" title="All the Year Round">All the Year Round</a>, 1 December 1860 to 3 August 1861)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Our_Mutual_Friend" title="Our Mutual Friend">Our Mutual Friend</a></i> (monthly serial, May 1864 to November 1865)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Signal-Man" title="The Signal-Man">The Signal-Man</a></i> (1866), first published as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mugby_Junction" title="Mugby Junction">Mugby Junction</a></i> collection in the 1866 Christmas edition of <i><a href="/wiki/All_the_Year_Round" title="All the Year Round">All the Year Round</a></i>.</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Mystery_of_Edwin_Drood" title="The Mystery of Edwin Drood">Edwin Drood</a></i> (<i>The Mystery of Edwin Drood</i>; monthly serial, April 1870 to September 1870), left unfinished due to Dickens\'s death</li></ul>\n<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/30px-Office-book.svg.png" width="30" height="30" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/45px-Office-book.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/60px-Office-book.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><ul style="font-weight: bold">\n<li><a href="/wiki/Book:Charles_Dickens" title="Book:Charles Dickens">Book: Charles Dickens</a></li>\n</ul></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="noprint metadata navbox" role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" style="font-weight:bold;padding:0.4em 2em"><ul style="margin:0.1em 0 0"><li style="display:inline"><span style="display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap"><span style="margin:0 0.5em"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Charles Dickens 3.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Charles_Dickens_3.jpg/22px-Charles_Dickens_3.jpg" width="22" height="21" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Charles_Dickens_3.jpg/33px-Charles_Dickens_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Charles_Dickens_3.jpg/43px-Charles_Dickens_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="299" data-file-height="290" /></a></span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Charles_Dickens" title="Portal:Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<p><b>Footnotes</b>\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em;
